Refuting the Mu'tazilite View on the Paralyzed Person's Movement
Maturidi critiques the Mu'tazilite view on the paralyzed person's movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Mu'tazilite claim that God creates the movement of a paralyzed person while the servant 'owns' a movement.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite paralyzed person argument falsely splits agency into two separate things.

Maturidi turns to a specific Mu'tazilite claim regarding the movement of a paralyzed person. The Mu'tazila argue that God creates the movement, while the servant has a distinct 'movement' that is a thing in itself, based on their ontology of thing-ness in the non-existent. Maturidi shows this leads to impossibility and confusion of aspects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
Read the passage to grasp the Mu'tazilite claim: God creates the movement, the servant has a movement, and that movement is a thing in itself.
Their concept of thing-ness applies to non-existents.
2
See Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi argues that this view is impossible because no parallel exists in creation. Identify his reasoning.
3
Note the concepts of thing-ness and aspect
Understand the role of 'thing-ness' (شيئية) in the Mu'tazilite argument and Maturidi's critique that they confuse aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقدير بفعل العباد أن لا يكون فعل واحد في الحقيقة لا اثنين ، أو يظن أن القول يوجب الشركة ، فجواب الحرف الأول في تقسيم القول لما اختلف فيه ، فعندنا أن فعل الله تعالى في الحقيقة غير فعل العبد ، وفعل العبد مفعوله لا فعله ، ووجود مثله فى الشاهد غير عسير نحو مد اثنين شيئا ينقطع ، وإزالة اثنين شيئا عن [وقبلها واحد؟] يسير به شركاء فيها إنه مفعولها في الحقيقة ، وكذلك المزال والمنقطع وكذلك الجمال فيه جزء لا يتجزى ، حمله اثنان قواهما واحد أن حقيقة فعلها وان اختلف فالمفعول واحد لهما ، فمثله الذي نحن فيه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله على أنه لا يجوز أن يملك أحد بقوته آخر على فعله ، ولا خلق فعل نفسه ، ولا أحد [يقدد؟] أن يفعل فعلا في غير حيزه وغير حال في نفسه ، فمن تقدير فعل الله بالموجود من فعل الخلق جهل ، وشبهه من جهة القدرة وقيام الفعل بالخلق. جل الله ذلك وتعالى . والقول الآخر قول من يقول ، إن خلق الشيء هو ذلك ، فقد بينا اختلاف الجهات في ذلك ، فجائز القول بالخلق من جهة هي غير جهة القول بالكفر ، على ما بينا من الشيئية . وقد زعم المعتزلة فى حركة المفلوج أنها لله خلقا وللعبد حركة ، وهى شيء لنفسها ؛ إذ الشيئية عندهم في المعدوم ، وهى دلالة حدث الجسم، وفى الكفر حجة الله على العبد فى التعذيب ودلالة سفهه في التحقيق . على أنا بينا أنه يحيل من حيث لا يكون مثل ذلك في الخلق ، وقد أوضحنا الفصل بين الأمرين ، وأن من قاس أحد الوجهين بالآخر فهو مغفل . على أن المعتزلة إذ لا الله إلى الخلق سوى أنه أوجد [هم] بعد أن لم يكونوا ، ولا ذلك معنى يجعلون من فعل العباد ، إنما هو معالجات وعناء وجهد ، والموجود فيهما نحن فيه مع المعنى الذى من العباد واقعان جميعا ، فلا وجه لإنكاره . ثم يقال فيما لا يكون مثله من العباد إحالته : أرأيت لو عارضك إخوانك فقالوا : تجعل للذي ذكرته أصلا ثم كون الجواهر بالخلق محال ، ثبت قدمها به ، وكون فعل لا ينفع فاعله ولا يدفع عنه الضرر ليس بحكمة ، فدل أن الذى صنع العالم انتفع به ، وقال : ما يوجب
The estimation by the actions of servants is that there is no action in reality by two, or one thinks that the statement necessitates partnership. So the answer to the first part is in dividing the statement on what is disputed. For us, the action of Allah, exalted, is in reality other than the action of the servant, and the action of the servant is His effect, not His action. And the existence of something similar in the witnessed world is not difficult: like two people dragging something that breaks, or two people removing something from its place, and [before it one?] weak so it is possible that they are partners in it, so it is their effect in reality. Similarly, the removed and the broken, and like a divisible atom, two people carry it with their strength combined, so the reality of their action differs but the effect is one for them. So the like of that is what we are in. And there is no power except through Allah, and it is not permissible for anyone to control another by his strength over his action, nor to create his own action, nor can anyone [can?] to perform an action outside his domain and state. So whoever estimates God's action from the found action of the creation is ignorant, and his doubt is from the aspect of power and the action's establishment in the creation. Exalted is Allah above that. The other statement is the statement of those who say that the creation of a thing is that thing itself. We have shown the difference of aspects in that, so it is permissible to say creation from an aspect other than the aspect of saying disbelief, as we have shown of thing-ness. The Mu'tazila claimed about the movement of a paralyzed person that it is from God as creation and from the servant as a movement, and it is a thing in itself, because thing-ness according to them is in the non-existent. It is evidence of the temporality of the body, and in disbelief it is God's proof against the servant in punishment and evidence of his folly in reality. We have shown that it is impossible because the like of that is not found in creation. We have clarified the distinction between the two matters, and whoever compares one aspect with the other is deluded. Since the Mu'tazila do not ascribe to God any creation except that He brought them into existence, and that is not the meaning... they make of the actions of servants only treatments, exertion, and effort. The existent in both is we in that with the meaning that is from the servants, both occurring, so there is no reason to deny it. Then it is said in what like of it is not found from the servants, its impossibility: Do you not see if your brethren objected to you, saying: You make for that which you mentioned an original, then the existence of atoms by creation is impossible, so their temporality is established by it, and the existence of an action that does not benefit its doer nor repel harm from him is not wise, so it indicates that the one who made the world benefited from it, and he said: what necessitates...
Source pages p. 837

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that an action cannot have two agents. Maturidi first addresses the estimation that no action is by two agents; he provides examples from the witnessed world: two people dragging a breaking stone, or two removing something where one is weak, yet the effect is one. Similarly, an indivisible atom carried by two persons with combined strength: their actions differ but the effect is one. Thus analogously, God's creative act and human acquisition can combine. He adds that no one controls another's action or creates outside his domain. Misapplying this leads to ignorance. Then he addresses the view that creation is identical to the created thing; he repeats that aspects differ. He then attacks the Mu'tazilite position on the paralyzed person: they claim God creates the movement and the servant has a movement, which is a thing in itself (following their ontology of thing-ness in non-existents). This movement serves as evidence of temporality and, in disbelief, as proof for punishment. Maturidi says this is impossible since no parallel exists in creation. He clarifies the distinction and warns against confusing aspects. He notes that the Mu'tazila only attribute existence to God, not actual actions of servants (which they call treatments and exertion). Since divine creation and human effort occur together, denial is unfounded. Finally, he anticipates a counter-argument: if this principle is set, then atoms cannot be created (so they are eternal), and an action that does not benefit the doer is unwise, implying the world's Maker must have benefited. (The argument breaks off.)

Key Terms
مفلوج
شيئية
معتزلة
تعذيب
سفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The movement of a paralyzed person is both created by God and owned by the servant as a separate entity.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such a dual attribution is impossible and confuses the aspects of creation and acquisition; no parallel exists in creation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the Mu'tazilite view on the paralyzed person's movement impossible?
A) Because God cannot create movement.
B) Because no parallel exists in creation.
C) Because the servant creates his own movement.
D) Because movement is not a thing.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite attempt to split the paralyzed person's movement into two distinct things is logically impossible, as it has no analogy in created existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever mentally split the act into 'God's creation' and 'your movement' to lessen your responsibility? How does Maturidi's argument expose the impossibility of such a split, and what does that demand of you right now?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought they could uphold divine justice by giving non-existent actions a 'thing-ness'—but Maturidi says this leads to logical absurdity. Where in your own beliefs do you hold onto comforting but logically inconsistent ideas about God's decree? Will you surrender them today?
?
If your every movement is entirely God's creation and yet you are truly accountable, how does that change the way you approach your next prayer, your next glance, your next word? Are you living as if your actions are truly yours before God?

Distinguishing Divine Creation from Human Action
Maturidi distinguishes divine creation from human action and rebuts a counter-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between God's creative act and human effort, and why comparing them is fallacious.

Core Idea
Creation and human action differ in aspect; confusing them leads to ignorance.

Maturidi further clarifies that creation is not identical to the created thing; they are distinguished by aspect. He criticizes those who compare God's action to human action, noting that human actions are merely 'treatments, exertion, and effort'—not creations. He also anticipates a counter-argument regarding atoms and divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Mu'tazilite view of creation
Read the passage where Maturidi discusses the Mu'tazilite reduction of God's role to mere existence-giving and human actions to exertion.
2
Understand the distinction of aspects
Note how Maturidi insists on the difference between creation and the created thing, and how aspects differ.
3
Recognize that human actions are not creations
Maturidi states that human actions are treatments and exertion, not creations. Understand why this matters for the debate.
4
Consider the counter-argument
Read the beginning of the counter-argument about atoms and benefit. Though incomplete, identify the logic: if this principle is set, then atoms cannot be created (so eternal) and actions that do not benefit are unwise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Divine Decree

Refuting Impossibility and Establishing Divine Oneness
Maturidi refutes the impossibility claim and cites Muslim consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the claim that creation from nothing is impossible, and how he presents the Muslim consensus as sufficient evidence for Tawhid.

Core Idea
The claim that creation is impossible leads to heresy; Muslim consensus affirms God as Creator.

Maturidi begins by attacking a fundamental objection: that bringing something into existence from nothing is impossible. He connects this claim to the position of heretics who assert the eternity of the world. Then he turns to the positive evidence: the agreement of Muslims that God is the Creator of all, which itself suffices as proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two claims
The passage contains a refutation and an affirmation. Find where Maturidi rejects the impossibility claim and where he asserts the Muslim consensus as evidence.
Notice the linking phrase 'وإذا كان دعوى الإحالة...'
2
Notice the link to heresy
Maturidi argues that the claim of impossibility (ihala) leads directly to the position of the Mu'tazila and the Dahriyya (materialists) who believe in the eternity of the world.
This shows the danger of the claim.
3
Understand the sufficient evidence
Maturidi states that the comprehensive agreement of Muslims that God is Creator, all else is created, and He is powerful over everything, is sufficient evidence without needing further detail.
This is a concise proof from consensus (ijma').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كون شيء لا من شيء خارج عن احتمال الخلق ، فمثله أمر الواحد الذى به كانا العالم . وإذا كان دعوى الإحالة توجب قول الزنادقة والدهرية في قدم العالم أظهر ذلك صدق من قال : الاعتزال طرف من الزندقة ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأما الدلالة ، فقد أوضحنا لمن عقل لو أنصف ما في جملة ما أدى مع المسلمون أن الله خالق وما سواه مخلوق ، وأنه قادر على كل شيء ، وهو رب كل شيء ، وألهه من غير اضطراب فى ذلك أو ميل قلب إلى خصوص ، في ذلك دليل كاف . وسنذكر أيضا بعض ما في ذلك . وأما القول بايجاب الضرورة فانه محال فاسد ؛ لأنه حسّى أن يعلم كل أنه مختار ، ولو جاز القول مما يعلمه كل على جهة قلبه الجاز ذلك فى جميع العالم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . فإن قلت : إذ لم توجب الضرورة دل أنه لا تدبير فيه لغيرك ، قيل : قد فرغنا عن دلالة ذلك ، ما مع أن يقال : هو من طريق الخلق اضطرار ، ولا صنع للعبد من ذلك الوجه ؛ يجوز إذ لا يسمى به ، ومن طريق الكسب اختيار ، فعلى ذلك تقسيم الأمر. ، وقد بيتنا . ألا تری أن قول الكفر كذب، وهو من حيث الدلالة على سفه القائل صدق ، فمثله يكون اختيارا من حيث الكسب ومن حيث الخلق لا ، وجهة الخلق لا تدفع عنه الاختيار بما ثبت ، فسواء لو كان خلق ذلك الفعل أو خلق السماء والأرض ؛ إذ ليس فى واحد صرف فعل الخلق عن الخلق ، ولا إزالة الاختيار ، فمثله خلق الأفعال ، ولا قوة إلا بالله على أن تسمية الخلق لا وصف الاضطرار ؛ إذ القدرة للفعل مخلوقة ، وهى سبب جعله مختارا لا مضطرا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The existence of something from nothing is outside the likelihood of creation. Likewise is the matter of the One by whom the world came to be. If the claim of impossibility necessitates the saying of the heretics and materialists about the eternity of the world, that shows the truth of whoever said: 'Mu'tazilism is a branch of heresy,' and there is no power except with God. As for the evidence, we have made clear to whoever has reason and is fair, in sum what led with the Muslims: that God is Creator and what is other than Him is created, and that He is powerful over everything, and He is Lord of everything, and God of everything, without disturbance in that or inclination of heart to any particular; in that is sufficient evidence. And we will also mention some of that. As for the saying that necessity is required, it is impossible and corrupt; because it is sensory that everyone knows that he is free, and if it were permissible to say concerning what everyone knows by way of his heart [?], that would be permissible in the entire world, and there is no power except with God. If you say: When necessity does not require, does it indicate that there is no disposing of it for other than you? It is said: We have finished with the indication of that, what is meant is that it is from the way of creation compulsion, and there is no act of the servant from that aspect; it is permissible because it is not named as such, and from the way of acquisition choice; upon that is the division of the matter, and we have explained. Do you not see that the statement of unbelief is false, but from the perspective of indicating the foolishness of the speaker it is true; likewise, it is choice from the aspect of acquisition and from the aspect of creation it is not, and the aspect of creation does not remove choice from it by what is established. So it is the same whether He created that act or created the heavens and the earth; for there is not in any one of them a diversion of the act of creation from creation, nor a removal of choice. Likewise is the creation of acts, and there is no power except with God. However, naming it creation is not a description of compulsion; because the capacity for the act is created, and it is the cause of making him free, not compelled, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 838

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument about divine creation and human action. Maturidi begins by linking the claim of impossibility (ihala) regarding creation to the position of heretics and materialists (dahriyya) who assert the eternity of the world. He then cites the Muslim consensus as sufficient evidence: God is Creator, all else is created, and He is powerful over everything. He then rejects the claim that human action is necessary (darura), stating that everyone sensory knows they are free. He addresses an objection: if necessity does not hold, does that mean no disposal by others? He responds that action is from one aspect creation (compulsion) and from another acquisition (choice). Maturidi explains that even a statement of unbelief is false in content but true in indicating the speaker's foolishness; similarly, an act is choice from the aspect of acquisition but not from creation. The aspect of creation does not negate choice. Finally, he emphasizes that calling the act 'creation' does not imply compulsion, because the capacity (qudra) for the act is created and that capacity makes the agent free, not compelled.

Key Terms
الإحالة
الضرورة
الزنادقة
الدهرية
الدلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creation from nothing is impossible; therefore the world is eternal.
Correction: Maturidi argues this claim leads to heresy, and the Muslim consensus on God creating from nothing is sufficient evidence.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the claim that human action is necessary (darura)?
A) Because necessity would imply that God is unjust.
B) Because every person knows by sensory experience that he is free (mukhtar).
C) Because the Quran explicitly denies necessity.
D) Because the Mu'tazila invented that claim.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the claim that creation is impossible by linking it to heresy and affirms Tawhid through the sufficient evidence of Muslim consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that some of your actions are beyond your control? Maturidi argues that you know by direct experience that you are free. How does denying that freedom affect your responsibility before Allah? Will you this week consciously take ownership of one choice you normally blame on circumstances?
?
Maturidi says that the collective Muslim understanding that Allah is the Creator is sufficient evidence. Do you rely on that certainty, or do you demand philosophical proofs? What would it take for you to rest in the fitra-based certainty of the Muslim Ummah?
?
The claim of impossibility in creation leads to heresy. Have you ever entertained thoughts that question Allah's power to create from nothing? How does that thought affect your worship? Repent of that assumption and affirm Allah's absolute power today.

Distinguishing Creation and Acquisition: Affirming Human Choice
Maturidi distinguishes creation from acquisition to affirm human choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between divine creation (khalq) and human acquisition (kash) and why creation does not negate human choice.

Core Idea
An act is created by God but acquired by the human; the created capacity makes the human free, not compelled.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses the central objection: if acts are created by God, how can humans be responsible? He responds by dividing acts into two aspects: creation (compulsion) and acquisition (choice). He explains that the aspect of creation does not remove choice, just as a false statement can be true in revealing the speaker's ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
The objection: if necessity is not required, does it imply no disposal by others? Maturidi responds by dividing action into creation and acquisition.
Notice the phrase 'من طريق الخلق اضطرار، ومن طريق الكسب اختيار'.
2
Study the analogy with a false statement
A statement of unbelief (kufr) is false in content but true in that it reveals the speaker's foolishness. Similarly, an act is choice from the aspect of acquisition but not from creation.
This illustrates how two aspects can coexist.
3
Understand why creation does not compel
Maturidi clarifies that calling an act 'created' does not imply compulsion because the capacity (qudra) for the act is itself created, and that capacity is what makes the agent free (mukhtar).
The created capacity is the cause of freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كون شيء لا من شيء خارج عن احتمال الخلق ، فمثله أمر الواحد الذى به كانا العالم . وإذا كان دعوى الإحالة توجب قول الزنادقة والدهرية في قدم العالم أظهر ذلك صدق من قال : الاعتزال طرف من الزندقة ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأما الدلالة ، فقد أوضحنا لمن عقل لو أنصف ما في جملة ما أدى مع المسلمون أن الله خالق وما سواه مخلوق ، وأنه قادر على كل شيء ، وهو رب كل شيء ، وألهه من غير اضطراب فى ذلك أو ميل قلب إلى خصوص ، في ذلك دليل كاف . وسنذكر أيضا بعض ما في ذلك . وأما القول بايجاب الضرورة فانه محال فاسد ؛ لأنه حسّى أن يعلم كل أنه مختار ، ولو جاز القول مما يعلمه كل على جهة قلبه الجاز ذلك فى جميع العالم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . فإن قلت : إذ لم توجب الضرورة دل أنه لا تدبير فيه لغيرك ، قيل : قد فرغنا عن دلالة ذلك ، ما مع أن يقال : هو من طريق الخلق اضطرار ، ولا صنع للعبد من ذلك الوجه ؛ يجوز إذ لا يسمى به ، ومن طريق الكسب اختيار ، فعلى ذلك تقسيم الأمر. ، وقد بيتنا . ألا تری أن قول الكفر كذب، وهو من حيث الدلالة على سفه القائل صدق ، فمثله يكون اختيارا من حيث الكسب ومن حيث الخلق لا ، وجهة الخلق لا تدفع عنه الاختيار بما ثبت ، فسواء لو كان خلق ذلك الفعل أو خلق السماء والأرض ؛ إذ ليس فى واحد صرف فعل الخلق عن الخلق ، ولا إزالة الاختيار ، فمثله خلق الأفعال ، ولا قوة إلا بالله على أن تسمية الخلق لا وصف الاضطرار ؛ إذ القدرة للفعل مخلوقة ، وهى سبب جعله مختارا لا مضطرا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The existence of something from nothing is outside the likelihood of creation. Likewise is the matter of the One by whom the world came to be. If the claim of impossibility necessitates the saying of the heretics and materialists about the eternity of the world, that shows the truth of whoever said: 'Mu'tazilism is a branch of heresy,' and there is no power except with God. As for the evidence, we have made clear to whoever has reason and is fair, in sum what led with the Muslims: that God is Creator and what is other than Him is created, and that He is powerful over everything, and He is Lord of everything, and God of everything, without disturbance in that or inclination of heart to any particular; in that is sufficient evidence. And we will also mention some of that. As for the saying that necessity is required, it is impossible and corrupt; because it is sensory that everyone knows that he is free, and if it were permissible to say concerning what everyone knows by way of his heart [?], that would be permissible in the entire world, and there is no power except with God. If you say: When necessity does not require, does it indicate that there is no disposing of it for other than you? It is said: We have finished with the indication of that, what is meant is that it is from the way of creation compulsion, and there is no act of the servant from that aspect; it is permissible because it is not named as such, and from the way of acquisition choice; upon that is the division of the matter, and we have explained. Do you not see that the statement of unbelief is false, but from the perspective of indicating the foolishness of the speaker it is true; likewise, it is choice from the aspect of acquisition and from the aspect of creation it is not, and the aspect of creation does not remove choice from it by what is established. So it is the same whether He created that act or created the heavens and the earth; for there is not in any one of them a diversion of the act of creation from creation, nor a removal of choice. Likewise is the creation of acts, and there is no power except with God. However, naming it creation is not a description of compulsion; because the capacity for the act is created, and it is the cause of making him free, not compelled, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 838

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument about divine creation and human action. Maturidi begins by linking the claim of impossibility (ihala) regarding creation to the position of heretics and materialists (dahriyya) who assert the eternity of the world. He then cites the Muslim consensus as sufficient evidence: God is Creator, all else is created, and He is powerful over everything. He then rejects the claim that human action is necessary (darura), stating that everyone sensory knows they are free. He addresses an objection: if necessity does not hold, does that mean no disposal by others? He responds that action is from one aspect creation (compulsion) and from another acquisition (choice). Maturidi explains that even a statement of unbelief is false in content but true in indicating the speaker's foolishness; similarly, an act is choice from the aspect of acquisition but not from creation. The aspect of creation does not negate choice. Finally, he emphasizes that calling the act 'creation' does not imply compulsion, because the capacity (qudra) for the act is created and that capacity makes the agent free, not compelled.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates an act, the human is compelled and has no choice.
Correction: Maturidi explains that creation (khalq) is one aspect, and acquisition (kasb) is another; creation does not remove choice because the capacity to act is created and that capacity makes the agent free.

?

Maturidi's Refutation of al-Ka'bi on Compulsion and Promise
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's claim of compulsion using aspects and promise/threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of al-Ka'bi's arguments for compulsion, showing that aspects (جهات) must be distinguished and that promise and threat imply human accountability.

Core Idea
Aspects distinguish freedom from compulsion; promise and threat assume choice.

Al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili theologian, argued that a free agent is compelled in the pain caused by his act. Maturidi responds using the principle of aspects (جهات): one cannot call the same thing both compelled and free without specifying how. He then shows that promise and threat, which al-Ka'bi also used to argue for compulsion, actually support human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the phrase 'الجهات' as it is key to the argument.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's claim
Al-Ka'bi claims that every free agent is compelled in the pain and suffering resulting from his own act. Notice how Maturidi reports this before responding.
This claim seems to deny that an act can be both free in doing and compelled in its consequences.
3
Understand the principle of aspects
Maturidi uses an analogy: knowing the same thing as both disbelief and faith, or as both accident and motion. You cannot call it known and unknown without specifying the aspect. Similarly, an act can be compelled in one aspect and free in another.
This principle (جهات) is foundational in Sunni kalam to resolve apparent contradictions.
4
Apply the principle to creation and punishment
Maturidi extends the principle to creation (خلق) and punishment (تعذيب). The same act can be created by God (compulsion in origin) but acquired by the human (free in responsibility).
This shows that al-Ka'bi's objection fails because he conflates different aspects.
5
Analyze the promise and threat argument
Al-Ka'bi also argued using divine promise and threat, but Maturidi counters that command and prohibition (which imply ability) are already established, so promise and threat actually confirm human choice, not compulsion.
This reinforces that accountability is meaningful only if humans have free choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قال الكعبى : إن كل مختار فى فعله مضطرا في تألمه به وتأذيه به ، فألزمه الأمرين فى الشيء الواحد ، وكذلك أن قد يجوز أن يعرف الفعل من لا يعرفه كفرا وايمانا ، أو شيئا عرضا ، وحركة وسكونا ، وهو ذلك بعينه ، ولم يجز فى الجملة أن يُقال : الذى يجهله هو الذى يعلمه ، والذي هو مضطر فيه هو الذي هو مختار فيه ، حتى يذكر معه الجهات ، فمثله في الخلق والتعذيب وغير ذلك ، ولا قوة إلا بالله واحتج بالوعد والوعيد بذلك ، وإذ ثبت الأمر والنهي ، وبان إغفاله في تقديره ، وظهر تمويهه ، فكذلك شأن الوعد والوعيد ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And al-Ka'bi said: Indeed every free agent in his act is compelled in his feeling pain by it and suffering by it, so he makes him subject to both matters in one thing. Likewise, it is possible that one who does not know an act knows it as disbelief and faith, or something accidental, motion and stillness, while it is the very same. And it is not permissible in general to say: that which he is ignorant of is that which he knows, and that in which he is compelled is that in which he is free, unless the aspects are mentioned along with it. Similarly in creation and punishment and other things, and there is no power except with God. And he argued by the promise and threat with that, and since command and prohibition are established, and his neglect in its estimation became clear, and his obfuscation appeared, so likewise is the matter of promise and threat, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 838

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ka'bi's objection and Maturidi's refutation. Al-Ka'bi claims that every free agent is compelled in his feeling pain and suffering from his own act, thus making one both free and compelled in one thing. Maturidi responds by comparing this to knowing the same thing as both faith and disbelief, or as both accident and motion—one must distinguish aspects (جهات). One cannot say the thing itself is both known and unknown, or both compelled and free, without specifying the aspects. Similarly, the act of creation and punishment must be considered under different aspects. Then Maturidi notes that al-Ka'bi also argued using promise and threat, but since command and prohibition (which imply human ability) are established, al-Ka'bi's neglect and obfuscation are clear, and promise and threat actually support human accountability, not compulsion.

Key Terms
الجهات
الوعد
الوعيد
الأمر والنهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God punishes a person for an act, the person must have been fully free; but al-Ka'bi's argument uses pain as compulsion.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that aspects differentiate: the act itself may be created by God, but the agent's acquisition is free, so punishment is just.

Checkpoint
What mistake does Maturidi point out in al-Ka'bi's claim about the free agent being compelled in his suffering?
A) Al-Ka'bi forgot that God is all-powerful.
B) Al-Ka'bi combined two different aspects (جهات) without distinguishing them.
C) Al-Ka'bi denied the existence of human choice altogether.
D) Al-Ka'bi relied on weak hadith.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi by showing that the same act can be both compelled and free under different aspects, and that promise and threat confirm human choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel pain or regret from a wrong choice you made, do you ever blame God or fate for that pain? How does Maturidi's teaching on aspects challenge you to see both God's creation and your own responsibility in that same act?
?
The promise of reward and threat of punishment assume you have real choice. Is your daily life shaped by that belief, or do you sometimes act as if you are compelled by circumstances? What one habit would you change to live more consistently with the fact that you are truly accountable?
?
If the same act can be known by God in one way and unknown by you in another, what does that say about how you should judge others' actions? Are you ready to give them the benefit of the aspects you don't see?

Refuting al-Ka'bi's Claim of Partnership in Action
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's claim using analogies and the principle of aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of al-Ka'bi's claim that an act cannot be both a human act and a divine creation without implying shirk, by recognizing the distinction of aspects (جهات) and using analogies from inheritance, ownership, and provision.

Core Idea
Dual attribution from different aspects does not imply partnership.

Al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili theologian, argued that if an act is both a human's action and God's creation, then they must be partners (shirk) in that act. Maturidi refutes this by pointing out that in everyday life we attribute the same thing to different agents from different perspectives without implying partnership. In this passage, he presents several analogies and establishes the principle of aspects (جهات) to show that divine and human agency are not conflicting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's argument structure come through.
Note the style of direct refutation.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's claim
What is al-Ka'bi's core argument that leads him to claim partnership (shirk) is necessary? How does he use the indivisibility of the act?
Look for 'محال أن يكون ذلك في الحقيقة فعلا لى خلقا لله' and 'يوجب الشركة المعقولة'.
3
Trace Maturidi's refutation
List the analogies Maturidi uses: inherited/purchased property, ownership by me and my slave, and attribution of food and clothing. How do these show that dual attribution from different aspects does not imply partnership?
Pay attention to the phrase 'جهات الفعل' at the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم زعم الكعبي أنه محال أن يكون ذلك في الحقيقة فعلا لى خلقا لله قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : وهذا الجهله للمحال ، وقد بينا بعض ذلك . ثم زعم أن ذا يوجب الشركة المعقولة ؛ إذ محال انفراد كل بجزء ، وإن كان لا يتجزى ، ثم عارض نفسه بقول الخصم أن ذلك يوجب فما كانت الجهة واحدة ، فأما فما اختلفت فلا . يعارض بملك ورث بعضه واشترى بعضه ثم عورض بملك لى ، ولعبد لى ، فأطنب في جواب ذلك ونحن نقول ، وبالله التوفيق : من تأمل الذى ذكر ، وله أدنى فهم ، ولا يكابر عقله علم سفهه ، وإن شاء استدل بالذي قدم من الميراث ليعلم جهله بالشركة الحاضرة ، فيكون ذلك عذرا في الجهل بما كان طريقه الاستدلال ؛ إذ خفى عليه حق العيان . لكن هذا سؤال لم يزل المعتزلة تظن أن ذلك يوجب ذلك . وإن كانوا لا يستحقون الجواب في ذلك ، فانا ننزع به عليهم ، فانهم قصدوا بالقول قول من يقول : خلق الشيء هو ذلك، ولا يوجد شيء واحد لاثنين في الشاهد لكل كله ؛ ولهذا الوجه أنكر أن يكون فعل واحد لاثنين ، فاذا لم يوجد له مثال يعلم أنه يوجب الاشتراك أو لا ، فقولهم يوجب ظن وخيال . ثم الأصل أن الفعل نفسه يجعلونه لله ملكا ، وكذلك للعبد ، وكذلك كل ملك لأجد فهو الله ملك وللعبد كذلك ، ولم يوجب ذلك شركا بينها في ملك الأفعال والأعيان ، فكيف فيما نحن فيه شركاء ، ثم يضاف إلى الله الإطعام والكسوة والرزق ، وذلك بعينه يضاف إلى الخلق ، ولا يوجب شركا ، فمثله الذي نحن فيه . مع ما بينا جهات الفعل بم لم يقل الفعل نفسه من تلك الجهات مشترك ؛ إذ كل جهة تحيط بالكل
Then al-Ka'bi claimed that it is impossible that that be in reality an act for [the human] and a creation for God. Shaykh Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: This is ignorance of the impossible, and we have explained some of that. Then he claimed that this necessitates rational partnership; because it is impossible for each to be alone with a part, even if it is indivisible. Then he contradicted himself by the opponent's saying that that necessitates when the aspect is one, but when it differs, it does not. He was opposed by [the analogy of] property, part of which was inherited and part purchased; then he was opposed by [the analogy of] ownership for me and for my slave; and he expatiated in answering that. And we say, and with God is success: Whoever ponders what was mentioned, and has the least understanding, and does not oppose his intellect, knows his foolishness. And if he wishes, he can infer by what was presented of inheritance to know his ignorance of present partnership, so that would be an excuse for ignorance of what is known by inference; since the truth of the apparent was hidden from him. But this question has not ceased: the Mu'tazila think that that necessitates that. Even if they do not deserve an answer in that, we will refute it against them; for they intended by this saying the saying of whoever says: 'The creation of the thing is that thing,' and there does not exist in the witnessed world one thing for two as a whole for each. For this reason they denied that one act could be for two. And if no example exists to know whether it necessitates partnership or not, their saying is based on opinion and imagination. Then the principle is that they make the very act a possession for God, and likewise for the servant; and likewise every possession for one is a possession for God and for the servant likewise, and that does not necessitate partnership between them in the possession of acts and entities. So how would it be in what we are discussing? Then feeding, clothing, and provision are attributed to God, and the very same are attributed to creation, and that does not necessitate partnership. So likewise is what we are discussing. Along with what we have explained of the aspects of the act: why is the act itself not said to be shared from those aspects? Because each aspect encompasses the whole.
Source pages p. 839

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ka'bi's claim that an act cannot be both a human act and a divine creation because that would imply partnership (shirk) in the act. Maturidi responds by calling it ignorance. Al-Ka'bi argued that if the act is indivisible, each agent cannot have a part. Maturidi notes that al-Ka'bi contradicted himself by admitting that aspects (جهات) differentiate. Then Maturidi gives analogies: inherited and purchased property can belong to one person from different aspects; likewise, ownership by me and my slave (dual attribution) does not imply partnership in possession. He then argues that the Mu'tazila think their question is valid, but it is based on the false assumption that if something is created by God, it cannot also belong to the human. Maturidi points out that in the witnessed world, we see things like food and clothing attributed both to God (as provider) and to humans (as givers) without partnership. He concludes that the act itself is not called shared because each aspect (divine creation and human acquisition) encompasses the whole act from its own perspective.

Key Terms
الشركة
الجهات
الملك
الإطعام
الكسوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates an act, then the human cannot truly be the agent; dual attribution implies partnership.
Correction: Maturidi shows through the example of provision and other analogies that dual attribution from different aspects does not necessitate partnership.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi use to refute the claim that dual attribution of an act implies partnership?
A) The analogy of a slave and a free man.
B) The analogy of feeding, clothing, and provision being attributed to both God and humans.
C) The analogy of inheritance and purchase.
D) The analogy of a king and a subject.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that attributing an act to both God and human from different aspects does not imply partnership, as seen in everyday analogies like provision and ownership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something, do you feel that attributing it fully to God diminishes your own effort? How does Maturidi's teaching on different aspects challenge that feeling, and what would change in your gratitude and humility if you truly internalized it?
?
Have you ever blamed God's decree for a sin you committed, thinking 'God created the act, so it's not my fault'? How does this lesson's distinction between divine creation and human acquisition force you to take full responsibility for your choices while still relying on God's mercy?
?
In your daily life, do you see the provision you earn as solely your doing, or do you acknowledge God as the ultimate Provider? What concrete habit could you adopt to live by the truth that the very same act of earning is both your action and God's creation, without partnership?

Maturidi's Response to al-Ka'bi on Blame for Sin
Maturidi responds to al-Ka'bi's claim that the agent of sin is more deserving of blame than the creator of sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that the doer of sin is more deserving of blame than its creator, by distinguishing between aspects of an act.

Core Idea
Blame attaches to specific aspects of an act, not to all attributes indiscriminately.

This passage from Kitab al-Tawhid presents a sharp theological exchange. Al-Ka'bi objects that if Allah creates sin, He would be blameworthy. Maturidi responds by pointing out that the same act has multiple aspects—thingness, movement, temporality—and blame applies only to the aspect that is sin, not to every attribute. This protects divine justice while affirming human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Al-Ka'bi asks why the human agent is more blameworthy than the divine creator of the act. Recognize the challenge this poses to divine justice.
The objection assumes that if Allah creates the act, He shares in the blame.
2
Analyze the response
Maturidi retorts with a counter-question: what makes the aspect of sin more deserving of blame than other aspects like thingness or movement? He argues that if blame followed from any aspect, it would follow from all, leading to absurd conclusions.
The absurdity: requiring blood for an act of faith or a good deed.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Since aspects differ, blame attaches only to the specific aspect of sin, and Allah's wisdom assigns rulings appropriately. The human agent is blameworthy for the sin aspect, not for the act's existence.
This preserves both divine justice and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الكعبى : ما جعل فاعل المعصية أحق بالذنب من خالقها * قيل له : وما جعل جهة المعصية أحق بالذم من جهة الشيئية والحركة والحدثية والعرضية ، وأنه خلاف للعبد ولله وغير لهما ، وأنه حجة الله ، ودليل سفه الكافر ؛ فإن الذم الشيء من ذلك لزمه الذم بكل مسمى به ، فيجب الدم على فعل الإيمان وكل حسن ، وإن لم يجب ثبت لذلك جهات ، يصرف إلى كل ما يليق به. الله تعالى حكمة من حيث جعله في الحقيقة قبيحا وسفها وجورا ثم
Then al-Ka'bi said: 'What makes the doer of disobedience more deserving of sin than its creator?' It was said to him: 'And what makes the aspect of disobedience more deserving of blame than the aspect of thingness, movement, temporality, and accidentalness? And that it is contradictory to the servant and to Allah and other than them? And that it is the proof of Allah and the evidence of the foolishness of the disbeliever? For if blame of something follows from that, then blame would follow from every attribute by which it is named, so blood would be required for the act of faith and every good deed. And if it is not required, then aspects are established for that, to be directed to what befits each. Allah the Exalted has wisdom in that He made it in reality ugly, foolish, and unjust. Then...'
Source pages p. 841

Commentary
This passage contains al-Ka'bi's objection and the response. Al-Ka'bi asks: what makes the agent of sin more deserving of blame than the creator of that sin (i.e., Allah)? The response is a rhetorical question: what makes the aspect of sin more deserving of blame than other aspects like thingness, movement, etc.? The argument is that if blame follows from any aspect, it would follow from all aspects, leading to absurd conclusions (e.g., requiring blood for good deeds). Since that is not the case, each aspect has its own ruling. The response concludes that Allah's wisdom determines what is actually ugly, foolish, and unjust.

Key Terms
فاعل المعصية
خالقها
جهة الذم
شيئية
حدثية
عرضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah creates the act of sin, He is blameworthy for that sin.
Correction: Blame attaches to the specific moral aspect of sin (which the human agent chooses), not to the act's existence created by Allah. Thus, Allah is not blameworthy.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the response to al-Ka'bi's objection?
A) That Allah creates sin but is not blameworthy because He is the Creator.
B) That the aspects of an act are multiple, and blame attaches to specific aspects, not all.
C) That the agent of sin is always more blameworthy than the creator.
D) That sin is not really sin from Allah's perspective.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defends divine justice by arguing that blame attaches to specific moral aspects of an act, not to its creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself mentally shifting some of the blame onto Allah—'He created my circumstances, my desires, my weakness'? How does this lesson force you to own your sin fully, without any shadow of excuse?
?
If blame attaches only to the specific moral quality you choose, not to the existence of your act, what does that mean for the sins you habitually commit? Are you ready to see them as your choices alone, and to repent with that full ownership?
?
This passage reveals that Allah's wisdom has made sin 'in reality ugly, foolish, and unjust.' Have you trained your heart to feel the ugliness of your own disobedience, or do you still find ways to dress it up as understandable? What would it cost you to see it as Allah sees it?

The Mu'tazila's Denial: Principle and Origin
Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila denial based on the term 'Creator' and their principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's fundamental principle that led them to deny God's creation of human actions, and how Maturidi critiques this by highlighting the magnifying meaning of 'Creator'.

Core Idea
Denying God's creation contradicts the very meaning of 'Creator' as magnification.

Maturidi begins by showing that the term 'Creator' (خالق) is used to magnify God, so a general application is more eloquent. Those who deny that God creates all human acts contradict this linguistic and theological principle. The Mu'tazila's denial stems from an empirical claim: they do not witness any act bringing something from non-existence to existence in the created world. Let's see how this unfolds in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, focusing on the first two sentences. Notice how Maturidi connects the word 'Creator' with magnification.
The word تعظيم (magnification) is key.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's principle
Look for the phrase 'لم يجدوا فعل أحد يخرجه غيره من العدم إلى الوجود'. This is their core objection: they only see acts of bringing together or separating, not true creation from nothing.
3
Understand the consequence
Because of this principle, they deny that God creates the essences of things (خلق الأعيان), and by extension, human acts. Maturidi points out that this denial is inconsistent with the magnifying meaning of 'Creator'.
The passage ends with 'وبمثله أنكرت المعتزلة خلق الأفعال'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن القول بالخالق يخرج مخرج التعظيم ، فكل ما هو أعم فهو أبلغ ، وبقائل لا ؛ لذلك اختلافا فنذكر معانى إنكاره أيضا . ثم الأصل أن انكار المعتزلة هذا بما لم يجدوا فعل أحد يخرجه غيره من العدم إلى الوجود ، وهو الأصل الذي له أنكر من أنكر خلق الأعيان بامتناعه فى الشاهد عن الوجود فى الحقيقة بفعل أحد ، بل فيه غير جمع وتفريق، فأبوا أن يكون خلق أعيان الأشياء بذلك ، وبمثله أنكرت المعتزلة خلق الأفعال ؛ فلذلك نسبهم الأوائل إلى ذلك ، مع ما قولهم في التحقيق ذلك ؛ لأنهم حققوا الأشياء في القدم، وجعلوا من الله ايجادها لا إحداث شيئيتها . وكانت الشيئية" لا به ، فيكون العالم عندهم في التحقيق حدثا أشياء ، لا أنه عن من أحدث عن غير شيء ، ثم ذكروا فى الكفر / والإيمان أنهما شيئان ، كان الفاعل إيجادهما ، لا جعلها شيئين ، فصارا من حيث الشيئية ليس للعبد . ثم لا ينكر ذلك ، فما ينكر أن يكون من حيث الشيئية خلقا ، ولا يدفع ذلك ، ولم يوجب بذلك أنه عذب لا لشيء ، ولا أنه عذب للشيئية، ولا أحيل التعذيب إذا سقطت عنه الشيئية" ، ولا أوجب الشرك بين الفاعل والشيئية في العقل [و]فى الوجود ، ولا أطلق القول بأنه لإثنين ؛ إذ هو بكليته فى أنه شيء ليس له ، وفى أنه إيمان وكفر له ، وكذا هذا التقرير فى حركة المفلوج ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also that the statement about the Creator is put forth in a context of magnification, so whatever is more general is more eloquent, and the one who says 'no' is therefore differing. So we mention the meanings of his denial as well. Then the principle is that the Mu'tazila's denial of this is because they did not find anyone who brings something else from non-existence to existence, and that is the principle by which the one who denied the creation of entities denied, due to its impossibility in the witnessed world of existence in reality by the act of anyone, rather it involves only gathering and separating. So they refused that the creation of the essences of things is by that, and similarly the Mu'tazila denied the creation of acts; therefore the early scholars attributed that to them, along with what their statement is in reality; because they posited things as pre-eternal, and made from God their bringing into existence, not the origination of their thingness. And thingness is not by Him, so the world in their view is in reality a happening of things, not that it is from something originated from nothing. Then they mentioned regarding unbelief and faith that they are two things, and the agent is their bringing into existence, not making them two things, so they become, in terms of thingness, not belonging to the servant. Then one does not deny that, so why would he deny that in terms of thingness it is creation, and he does not repel that, and that does not necessitate that He punishes for no reason, nor that He punishes for thingness, nor is punishment deemed impossible if thingness is removed, nor does it necessitate association between the agent and thingness in intellect and in existence, nor does it imply the statement that it is from two; for it is entirely that it is a thing not his, and in that it is faith and unbelief for him. And such is this explanation concerning the movement of the paralyzed, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 841

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's denial that God creates human actions. Maturidi begins by noting that the term 'Creator' is used for magnification, so a more general term is more emphatic; those who deny that God creates all things are contradicting this. The root of the Mu'tazila's denial is that they do not see anyone in the witnessed world bringing something from non-existence to existence; they only see bringing together or separating existing things. Therefore they reject that God creates the essences of things directly, and by extension they deny that God creates human acts. Maturidi explains that in reality, the Mu'tazila posit that things (including the essences of acts) are pre-eternal (qadim), and God only brings them into existence, not originates their thingness. This leads to a contradiction: they consider faith and unbelief as things, but the servant does not own their thingness—God brings them into existence. Yet they do not deny that God creates them in terms of thingness, but they do not call it 'creation'. Maturidi argues that this does not lead to absurdities like punishment without reason or associating partners with God. The example of the paralyzed person's movement illustrates that the act can be attributed both to God (as creator of its thingness) and to the servant (as its acquirer).

Key Terms
خالق (Creator)
تعظيم (magnification)
خلق الأعيان (creation of entities)
جمع وتفريق (gathering and separating)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila deny God's power over human actions entirely, but their reasoning is based on a limited view of causality in the witnessed world.
Correction: Maturidi shows that their denial stems from a philosophical principle, not from outright rejection of divine power.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the principle underlying the Mu'tazila's denial of God creating human actions?
A) They believe humans are completely independent in their actions.
B) They did not find any act in the witnessed world that brings something from non-existence to existence.
C) They think God only creates good actions.
D) They reject the concept of thingness.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's denial of God creating human acts is rooted in their empirical principle, which Maturidi exposes as contradictory to the magnifying connotation of 'Creator'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems to have purely natural causes, do you ever unconsciously limit God's creative power in your heart? How does this lesson's critique of the Mu'tazila's empiricism challenge you to see God's hand even in ordinary causes?
?
Are you comfortable with the idea that God creates even your actions—including your doubts and failures? How does that affect your sense of accountability and your trust in God's ultimate control?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected divine creation of human acts because they couldn't observe it. In what areas of your faith do you similarly rely on what you can see or understand, rather than accepting what revelation teaches? Commit to one area where you will surrender your empiricism to divine truth.

Thingness and the Mu'tazila's Contradiction
Maturidi explains and refutes the Mu'tazila concept of thingness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila concept of thingness (شيئية) as pre-eternal, and how Maturidi refutes it by showing that it leads to logical inconsistencies regarding faith, unbelief, and punishment.

Core Idea
If thingness is not from God, then acts are not truly created, but this leads to absurdities in divine justice.

Having exposed the Mu'tazila's principle, Maturidi now delves deeper into their actual position: they consider the 'thingness' of entities and acts as pre-eternal, with God only bringing them into existence. This has serious implications for how we understand faith, unbelief, and divine punishment. Let's trace Maturidi's argument as he unpacks these consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the Arabic
Begin from 'فذلك نسبهم الأوائل إلى ذلك' up to the end. Note the introduction of the term شيئية (thingness).
The term شيئية is crucial; it refers to the intrinsic property of being a 'thing'.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila view of thingness
Maturidi explains that the Mu'tazila made things pre-eternal (في القدم) in their thingness, and God only brings them into existence (ايجاد) without originating their thingness. This means the world is a 'happening of things' (حدثا أشياء) rather than creation from nothing.
3
Analyze the consequence for faith and unbelief
The Mu'tazila consider faith and unbelief as 'two things' (شيئان) whose existence is brought about by God, but their thingness is not from God. Maturidi argues this leads to ambiguity: if thingness is not from God, then God is not the creator of the actions in a full sense, yet they still attribute faith/unbelief to the servant.
4
Examine Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi questions why the Mu'tazila would deny that thingness is creation, and argues that their view does not avoid the perceived absurdities (like punishment without reason, or associating partners with God). He uses the analogy of a paralyzed person's movement to illustrate.
The phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' is a pious conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And also that the statement about the Creator is put forth in a context of magnification, so whatever is more general is more eloquent, and the one who says 'no' is therefore differing. So we mention the meanings of his denial as well. Then the principle is that the Mu'tazila's denial of this is because they did not find anyone who brings something else from non-existence to existence, and that is the principle by which the one who denied the creation of entities denied, due to its impossibility in the witnessed world of existence in reality by the act of anyone, rather it involves only gathering and separating. So they refused that the creation of the essences of things is by that, and similarly the Mu'tazila denied the creation of acts; therefore the early scholars attributed that to them, along with what their statement is in reality; because they posited things as pre-eternal, and made from God their bringing into existence, not the origination of their thingness. Then one does not deny that, so why would he deny that in terms of thingness it is creation, and he does not repel that, and that does not necessitate that He punishes for no reason, nor that He punishes for thingness, nor is punishment deemed impossible if thingness is removed, nor does it necessitate association between the agent and thingness in intellect and in existence, nor does it imply the statement that it is from two; for it is entirely that it is a thing not his, and in that it is faith and unbelief for him. شيئية (thingness)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Mu'tazila's concept of thingness completely denies divine involvement in human actions.
Correction: Actually, they affirm God brings the action into existence, but they deny that He creates its 'thingness', a subtle distinction.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the Mu'tazila's view on the 'thingness' of faith and unbelief?
A) Thingness is created by God directly.
B) Thingness is pre-eternal and not created by God.
C) Thingness is created by the servant.
D) Thingness does not exist for faith and unbelief.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's view that thingness is pre-eternal leads to internal contradictions and fails to provide a coherent account of divine justice and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every action—including your sins and good deeds—has its very 'thingness' (essential nature) created by God, how would that change the way you view your own faults and failures? Would you still feel entitled to blame others or circumstances? What would it mean to repent while acknowledging that even the capacity to sin is from God?
?
The Mu'tazila feared that attributing the essence of evil acts to God would make Him unjust. Do you sometimes avoid taking full responsibility for your actions by subtly placing the 'thingness' of your mistakes outside of God's control? How does accepting that even the nature of your wrong choices is from God affect your understanding of His justice and mercy?
?
Consider the paralyzed person analogy: the movement is attributed to the person, but its thingness is from God. In your life, what areas do you still claim as 'your own' in terms of essence—like your talents, your successes, or even your righteous deeds? Are you willing to say that everything you are and have is, in its very nature, from God alone? What would change if you lived that conviction daily?

Al-Ka'bi's Objection and the Dual Aspect of Acts
Maturidi's response to al-Ka'bi on sin and blame through aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi defends divine transcendence by distinguishing between the multiple aspects of an act, showing that blame follows the human aspect, not the divine.

Core Idea
Acts have multiple aspects; blame attaches only to the human side, not to God.

Al-Ka'bi raises a powerful objection: if God creates sin, He deserves more blame than the human doer. Maturidi answers by examining the different 'aspects' or 'directions' (جهات) of an act. The passage we will read introduces this key distinction and defends God's wisdom in creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection's force and Maturidi's initial counter-question about aspects.
The text begins with 'Then al-Ka'bi said...'
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on 'جهة' (aspect) and how it is used to redirect blame away from God.
The passage mentions several aspects: thingness, movement, contingency, accidentalness.
3
Analyze the analogy
Examine the analogy about knowing and informing about the disbeliever's act. How does it illustrate the dual aspect?
One who describes the act from the disbeliever's perspective is lying; one who describes it as it truly is (a sin) is truthful.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that God creates the act as a wise and just creation, while the human acquires the sin aspect.
This preserves both God's transcendence and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الكعبى : ما جعل فاعل المعصية أحق بالذنب من خالقها * قيل له : وما جعل جهة المعصية أحق بالذم من جهة الشيئية والحركة والحدثية والعرضية ، وأنه خلاف للعبد ولله وغير لهما ، وأنه حجة الله ، ودليل سفه الكافر ؛ فإن الذم الشيء من ذلك لزمه الذم بكل مسمى به ، فيجب الدم على فعل الإيمان وكل حسن ، وإن لم يجب ثبت لذلك جهات ، يصرف إلى كل ما يليق به. الله تعالى حكمة من حيث جعله في الحقيقة قبيحا وسفها وجورا ثم الذي من  ومن كان جاهلا ومن كتاب الترحيد ٢٤٣ رمذموما ، وهو من هذا الوجه حق وحكمة ، والفعل من حيث العبد سفه وجوز ، ذلك الوجه قبيح ومعصية . ألا ترى أن من عرف فعل الكافر على ما هو عنده أخير به كان كاذبا ، ومن عرفه على ما عليه حقيقته كان عالما احكيما ، ولو أخبر به كان صادقا ، فعلى ذلك خلق الله ذلك وجعله على ما هو عليه ، وفعل العبد [لا] .
Then al-Ka'bi said: What makes the doer of the sin more deserving of the sin than its creator? It was said to him: And what makes the aspect of sin more deserving of blame than the aspect of thingness, movement, contingency, and accidentalness? And that it is different for the servant and for Allah, and other than both of them, and that it is the proof of Allah and evidence of the foolishness of the disbeliever; for if blame of a thing from that is necessary, then blame by every name attributed to it would be necessary, so blood would be required for the act of faith and every good act, and if it is not necessary, then aspects are established for it, directed to whatever befits it. Allah—exalted—is wisdom in terms of His making it in reality ugly, foolish, and unjust. Then the one who from [PAGE_842] and whoever is ignorant and from the Book of Tawhid, the one who is blamed, and from this aspect it is truth and wisdom, and the act from the aspect of the servant is foolishness and injustice, that aspect is ugly and a sin. Do you not see that whoever knows the act of the disbeliever as it is according to him and informs of it is lying, and whoever knows it as it is in its reality is a knowing and wise person, and if he informs of it he is truthful; upon that, Allah created that and made it as it is, and the act of the servant [is not]?
Source pages p. 841

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer: al-Ka'bi objects that if God creates sin, then He would be more deserving of blame than the human doer. Maturidi's response distinguishes aspects of the act. He argues that why single out the aspect of sin over the aspects of thingness, movement, contingency, etc.? The act has multiple aspects, and blame is directed appropriately. From God's perspective, the act is wise and just—He creates it as a test, evidence, etc. From the human perspective, it is ugly and sinful. Maturidi gives an analogy: if someone describes the disbeliever's act from the disbeliever's perspective, he is lying about its true nature; if he describes it as it is in reality (i.e., that it is a sin), he is truthful. Thus, God creates the act as it is, but the attribute of sin belongs to the human acquisition. The human act is not negated; rather, the human truly performs it.

Key Terms
جهة (aspect)
ذنب (sin)
ذم (blame)
حكمة (wisdom)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates sin, He is directly blameworthy for it.
Correction: Blameworthiness follows the human aspect (acquisition), not the divine aspect (creation). God's creation is wise and just.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi argue that God is not blameworthy for creating sin?
A) God creates sin but humans choose it freely.
B) The act has multiple aspects; the sin aspect belongs to the human, while the divine aspect is wisdom and justice.
C) God does not actually create sin.
D) Blame is only for the creator, not the doer.

Key Takeaway
God's creation of acts is wise; human acquisition of sin makes them blameworthy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever feel a subtle resentment toward God for creating you in that situation? After reading this lesson, how does the distinction between God's wise creation and your own sinful acquisition challenge you to take full responsibility for your actions? Will you now, when tempted to blame circumstances, consciously redirect that blame to yourself?
?
Imagine you are about to sin and you think: 'God is the one creating this act, so it's not really my fault.' How does Maturidi's argument about aspects expose the lie in that thought? What specific commitment can you make today to stop using God's decree as an excuse for your sins?
?
The passage compares knowing the disbeliever's act from his own perspective (a lie) versus knowing it as it truly is (a sin). Are you honest with yourself about the ugliness of your own sins, or do you rationalize them? What would it mean for you to 'know it as it is in its reality' and respond with repentance?

Refuting Objections on Divine Creation and Human Choice
Maturidi refutes an objection against divine creation of actions by reaffirming human choice and moral accountability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that God's creation of actions negates human responsibility, by affirming that the servant's act is real and chosen, and that God's creation does not imply compulsion.

Core Idea
Divine creation of actions does not compel—the servant still chooses, making moral accountability meaningful.

Maturidi continues his defense of the Ash'ari position on qadar by addressing a further objection: if God creates all actions, then why call anyone else 'creator'? He responds by returning to the established principle that the servant has a real act and choice. In this passage, he clarifies that God's knowledge, predestination, and naming of the act do not negate human agency—rather, they make command, prohibition, punishment, and reward consistent. He warns that those who deny this are following an illusion born of ignorance of divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the objection-and-response structure.
2
Identify the key principle
Maturidi states that the servant has a real act and that he chooses it. This is the foundation for rebutting the objection.
The term 'حقيقة' (reality) is crucial.
3
Understand the non-compulsion argument
God's creation of the act does not push, compel, or coerce the servant. This is essential for preserving moral responsibility.
Look for the words 'لم يدفعه', 'لم يحمله', 'لم يضطره'.
4
Connect to moral accountability
Because there is no compulsion, command, prohibition, punishment, and reward are meaningful. Reflect on how this counters the objection.
Maturidi lists divine actions that coexist with human choice.
5
Consider the concluding warning
Maturidi warns that denying God's creation based on such objections is ignorance of wisdom. The passage ends with a call to submission.
The final phrases may be ambiguous but emphasize reliance on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يعارض تسمية غير الذى خلق الموت وأحوال الخلق أحق من الذي خلق ، وهو بالجملة مُسمّى أنه خالق ذلك ، فمهما قال في ذلك فهو جواب له فى الأول : والأصل أنه ثبت للعبد فعل فى الحقيقة ، وأنه له يختار ، وأنه آثر الأشياء عنده وأحبها ، وأن خلق ذلك لم يدفعه إليه ، ولم يحمله ولم يضطره إليه ، فوجود ذلك ووجود علمه به وخبره عنه وإثباته فى اللوح المحفوظ وايجاب معاداته لوقت فعله و تسميته بما سمى ، إذ لم يضطره إلى فعله ولا حمله عليه ، حسن معه الأمر والنهى والتعذيب والإثابة . ومن أنكر بهذا خلقه فتعلقه بهذا النوع خيال ، وحقه أن ينظر الوجه الذى به يعرف خلق الأشياء ، فإن أمكن تحقيقه فالإنكار بهذا النوع إنما هو جهل بالحكمة . وعلى ذلك كان أول ما جبل عليه فيعلم إن خضع للمكرم به إن شاء الله ، وإن لم يمكن تسقط المسألة ، ويفضل الذي عارض کله ، ولا قوة إلا بالله به
Then he counters the naming of other than the one who created death and the states of creation as more deserving than the one who created, and it is in summary that He is named Creator of that, so whatever he says in that, it is a response to him in the first: And the principle is that a real act is established for the servant, and that he chooses it, and that he prefers things according to him and loves them, and that the creation of that did not push it to him, nor compel him, nor coerce him to it. So the existence of that, and the existence of His knowledge of it, and His report of it, and its inscription in the Preserved Tablet, and the necessity of His enmity at the time of his act, and his naming it by what it is named, since He did not coerce him to his act nor compel him to it, makes good with it both command and prohibition, punishment and reward. Whoever denies His creation by this, his attachment to this type is illusion, and his right is to look at the aspect by which the creation of things is known, for if its verification is possible, then denial in this manner is only ignorance of wisdom. And upon that was the first thing he was created for, so he knows if he submits to the honored one if Allah wills, and if it is not possible, the question drops, and the one who objects is refuted entirely, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 842

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection that someone other than God (like an idol) might be more deserving of being called 'creator' of death and states of creation. Maturidi responds by reaffirming the principle: the servant has a real act and choice—he prefers and loves certain things. God's creation of the act does not push, compel, or coerce the servant into acting. Because God does not coerce, His knowledge, predestination, and naming of the act do not negate human responsibility; rather, they make command, prohibition, punishment, and reward meaningful. Those who deny God's creation based on this type of objection are clinging to an illusion. If one properly considers the aspect by which things are created, he will see that denial is due to ignorance of divine wisdom. The passage ends by noting that the first thing a person is created for is to submit to the honored (perhaps God or the Prophet), and if that is not realized, the question falls away and the objector is refuted.

Key Terms
اضطرار (compulsion)
اختيار (choice)
أمر ونهى (command and prohibition)
حكمة (wisdom)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates all actions, then humans are compelled and have no real choice.
Correction: Maturidi affirms that divine creation does not compel; the servant's act is real and chosen, which makes commands and punishments just.

Checkpoint
What makes command, prohibition, punishment, and reward valid according to Maturidi?
A) God creates the act without any human involvement.
B) The servant's act is real and chosen by him, not compelled by God.
C) Only good acts are created by God.
D) Humans create their own acts independently of God.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defends human responsibility by distinguishing divine creation from compulsion, showing that God's predestination does not negate genuine choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent sin you committed. If God created that act, did He compel you? Or did you choose it? How does the distinction between creation and compulsion change the way you view your own guilt and your need to repent?
?
Do you sometimes blame your circumstances or even divine decree for your failures? What would it mean to fully accept that your choices are real and that God's creation of your acts does not lessen your accountability? How would that change your daily effort to obey?
?
If you truly believe that God's creation of your actions does not force you, then why do you still hesitate to obey when you know what is right? What fear or excuse is holding you back, and what does this lesson say about that excuse?
